<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collected_papers_and_posthumous_publications">Collected papers and posthumous publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rachel_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Collected papers and posthumous publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carson bequeathed her manuscripts and papers to <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>, to take advantage of the new state-of-the-art preservations facilities of the <a href="/wiki/Beinecke_Rare_Book_and_Manuscript_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="Beinecke Rare Book and Manuscript Library">Beinecke Rare Book and Manuscript Library</a>. Her longtime agent and literary executor <a href="/wiki/Marie_Rodell" title="Marie Rodell">Marie Rodell</a> spent nearly two years organizing and cataloging Carson's papers and correspondence, distributing all the letters to their senders so that only what each correspondent approved would be submitted to the archive.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1965, Rodell arranged for the publication of an essay Carson had intended to expand into a book: <i>A Sense of Wonder</i>. The essay, which was combined with photographs by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_Pratt_II&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Charles Pratt II (page does not exist)">Charles Pratt</a> and others, exhorts parents to help their children experience the "...lasting pleasures of contact with the natural world [...] available to anyone who will place himself under the influence of earth, sea and sky and their amazing life."<sup id="cite_ref-Murphy_25_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy_25-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the letters in <i>Always Rachel</i>, in 1998 a volume of Carson's previously unpublished work was published as <i>Lost Woods: The Discovered Writing of Rachel Carson</i>, edited by Linda Lear. All of Carson's books remain in print.<sup id="cite_ref-Murphy_25_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy_25-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grassroots_environmentalism_and_the_EPA">Grassroots environmentalism and the EPA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rachel_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Grassroots environmentalism and the EPA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carson's work had a powerful impact on the environmental movement. <i>Silent Spring</i>, in particular, was a rallying point for the fledgling social movement in the 1960s. According to environmental engineer and Carson scholar H. Patricia Hynes, "<i>Silent Spring</i> altered the balance of power in the world. No one since would be able to sell pollution as the necessary underside of progress so easily or uncritically."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Carson's work, and the activism it inspired, are at least partly responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Deep_ecology" title="Deep ecology">deep ecology</a> movement, and the overall strength of the grassroots environmental movement since the 1960s. It was also influential on the rise of <a href="/wiki/Ecofeminism" title="Ecofeminism">ecofeminism</a> and on many feminist scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson's most direct legacy in the environmental movement was the campaign to ban the use of DDT in the United States (and related efforts to ban or limit its use throughout the world). Though environmental concerns about DDT had been considered by government agencies as early as Carson's testimony before the President's Science Advisory Committee, the 1967 formation of the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Defense_Fund" title="Environmental Defense Fund">Environmental Defense Fund</a> was the first major milestone in the campaign against DDT. The organization brought lawsuits against the government to "establish a citizen's right to a clean environment," and the arguments employed against DDT largely mirrored Carson's. By 1972, the Environmental Defense Fund and other activist groups had succeeded in securing a phase-out of DDT use in the United States (except in emergency cases).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The creation of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Nixon_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Nixon Administration">Nixon Administration</a> in 1970 addressed another concern that Carson had brought to light. Until then, the same agency (the <a href="/wiki/USDA" class="mw-redirect" title="USDA">USDA</a>) was responsible both for regulating pesticides and promoting the concerns of the agriculture industry; Carson saw this as a <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">conflict of interest</a>, since the agency was not responsible for effects on wildlife or other environmental concerns beyond farm policy. Fifteen years after its creation, one journalist described the EPA as "the extended shadow of <i>Silent Spring</i>." Much of the agency's early work, such as enforcing the 1972 <a href="/wiki/Federal_Insecticide,_Fungicide,_and_Rodenticide_Act" title="Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act">Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act</a>, was directly related to Carson's work.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1980s, the policies of the <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Reagan Administration">Reagan Administration</a> emphasized economic growth, rolling back many of the environmental policies adopted in response to Carson and her work.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticisms_of_environmentalism_and_DDT_restrictions">Criticisms of environmentalism and DDT restrictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rachel_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Criticisms of environmentalism and DDT restrictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carson and the environmental movement were, and continue to be, criticized by some who argue that restrictions placed on pesticides, specifically DDT, have caused tens of millions of needless deaths and hampered agriculture (and, implicitly, that Carson bears responsibility for inciting such restrictions).<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Conservative_criticism_of_Carson_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conservative_criticism_of_Carson-82">[82]</a></sup> These arguments have been dismissed as "outrageous" by former WHO scientist Socrates Litsios. May Berenbaum, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Illinois">University of Illinois</a> entomologist, says, "to blame environmentalists who oppose DDT for more deaths than Hitler is worse than irresponsible."<sup id="cite_ref-salon_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon-83">[83]</a></sup> Investigative journalist Adam Sarvana and others characterize this notion as a "myth" promoted principally by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bate" title="Roger Bate">Roger Bate</a> of the pro-DDT advocacy group <a href="/wiki/Africa_Fighting_Malaria" title="Africa Fighting Malaria">Africa Fighting Malaria</a> (AFM).<sup id="cite_ref-NRNS_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRNS-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guts_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guts-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 2000s, however, criticism of the real and alleged ban(s) of DDT her work prompted became much more intense.<sup id="cite_ref-quig_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quig-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Erik_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erik-87">[87]</a></sup> In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a> think tank <a href="/wiki/Competitive_Enterprise_Institute" title="Competitive Enterprise Institute">Competitive Enterprise Institute</a> set up a website asserting "Millions of people around the world suffer the painful and often deadly effects of malaria because one person sounded a false alarm. That person is Rachel Carson."<sup id="cite_ref-Erik_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erik-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Slate_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slate-88">[88]</a></sup> A 2012 review article in <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a> by Rob Dunn<sup id="cite_ref-dunn_2012_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dunn_2012-89">[89]</a></sup> commemorating the 50th anniversary of <i><a href="/wiki/Silent_Spring" title="Silent Spring">Silent Spring</a></i> prompted a response in a letter written by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Trewavas" title="Anthony Trewavas">Anthony Trewavas</a> and co-signed by 10 others, including <a href="/wiki/Chris_J._Leaver" title="Chris J. Leaver">Christopher Leaver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Ames" title="Bruce Ames">Bruce Ames</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_Tren&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Richard Tren (page does not exist)">Richard Tren</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lachmann" title="Peter Lachmann">Peter Lachmann</a>, who quote estimates of 60 to 80 million deaths "as a result of misguided fears based on poorly understood evidence."<sup id="cite_ref-trewavas_2012_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trewavas_2012-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biographer Hamilton Lytle believes these estimates unrealistic, even assuming that Carson can be "blamed" for worldwide DDT policies.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Quiggin" title="John Quiggin">John Quiggin</a> and Tim Lambert have written that "the most striking feature of the claim against Carson is the ease with which it can be refuted." DDT was never banned for anti-malarial use,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> (its ban for agricultural use in the United States in 1972 did not apply outside the US or to anti-malaria spraying;<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> the international treaty that banned most uses of DDT and other organochlorine pesticides—the 2001 <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Convention_on_Persistent_Organic_Pollutants" title="Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants">Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants</a>—included an exemption for DDT for the use of malaria control until affordable substitutes could be found.)<sup id="cite_ref-quig_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quig-86">[86]</a></sup> Mass outdoor spraying of DDT was abandoned in poor countries subject to malaria, such as Sri Lanka, in the 1970s and 1980s, not because of government prohibitions, but because the DDT had lost its ability to kill the mosquitoes<sup id="cite_ref-quig_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quig-86">[86]</a></sup> (because of insects' very short breeding cycle and large number of offspring, the most resistant insects that survive and pass on their genetic traits to their offspring replace the pesticide-slain insects relatively rapidly. Agricultural spraying of pesticides produces <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_resistance" title="Pesticide resistance">resistance</a> to the pesticide in seven to ten years).<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Consequently, some experts have argued that restrictions placed on the agricultural use of DDT have increased its effectiveness as a tool for battling malaria. According to pro-DDT advocate <a href="/wiki/Amir_Attaran" title="Amir Attaran">Amir Attaran</a> the result of the 2004 <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Stockholm Convention">Stockholm Convention</a> banning DDT's use in agriculture "is arguably better than the status quo ... For the first time, there is now an insecticide which is restricted to vector control only, meaning that the selection of resistant mosquitoes will be slower than before."<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Posthumous_honors">Posthumous honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rachel_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Posthumous honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A variety of groups ranging from government institutions to environmental and conservation organizations to scholarly societies have celebrated Carson's life and work since her death. Perhaps most significantly, on June 9, 1980, Carson was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>, the highest civilian honor in the United States<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> A 17¢ <a href="/wiki/Great_Americans_series" title="Great Americans series">Great Americans series</a> <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamp</a> was issued in her honor the following year; several other countries have since issued Carson postage as well.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HAER_PBG_9thStreet_361504pv.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/HAER_PBG_9thStreet_361504pv.jpg/220px-HAER_PBG_9thStreet_361504pv.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/HAER_PBG_9thStreet_361504pv.jpg/330px-HAER_PBG_9thStreet_361504pv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/HAER_PBG_9thStreet_361504pv.jpg/440px-HAER_PBG_9thStreet_361504pv.jpg 2x" data-file-width="946" data-file-height="628" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HAER_PBG_9thStreet_361504pv.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Rachel Carson Bridge in Pittsburgh, mid-1999</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carson's birthplace and childhood home in <a href="/wiki/Springdale,_Pennsylvania" title="Springdale, Pennsylvania">Springdale, Pennsylvania</a>—now known as the <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_Homestead" title="Rachel Carson Homestead">Rachel Carson Homestead</a>—became a <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> site, and the <a href="/wiki/Nonprofit" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonprofit">nonprofit</a> Rachel Carson Homestead Association was created in 1975 to manage it.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Her <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_House_(Colesville,_Maryland)" title="Rachel Carson House (Colesville, Maryland)">home</a> in <a href="/wiki/Colesville,_Maryland" title="Colesville, Maryland">Colesville, Maryland</a> where she wrote <i>Silent Spring</i> was named a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a> in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Near <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, a 35.7 miles (57&#160;km) hiking trail, called the <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_Trail" title="Rachel Carson Trail">Rachel Carson Trail</a> and maintained by the Rachel Carson Trails Conservancy, was dedicated to Carson in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> A Pittsburgh bridge was also renamed in Carson's honor as the <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_Bridge" title="Rachel Carson Bridge">Rachel Carson Bridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bridge_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bridge-101">[101]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Department_of_Environmental_Protection" title="Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Protection">Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Protection</a> State Office Building in <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Harrisburg">Harrisburg</a> is named in her honor. Elementary schools in <a href="/wiki/Gaithersburg,_Maryland" title="Gaithersburg, Maryland">Gaithersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Maryland" title="Montgomery County, Maryland">Montgomery County, Maryland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sammamish,_Washington" title="Sammamish, Washington">Sammamish, Washington</a><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/San_Jose,_California" title="San Jose, California">San Jose, California</a><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> were named in her honor, as were middle schools in <a href="/wiki/Beaverton,_Oregon" title="Beaverton, Oregon">Beaverton, Oregon</a><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Herndon,_Virginia" title="Herndon, Virginia">Herndon, Virginia</a><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_Middle_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Rachel Carson Middle School">Rachel Carson Middle School</a>), and a high school in <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Brooklyn, New York">Brooklyn, New York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two research vessels currently sail in the US bearing the name R/V <i>Rachel Carson</i>. One is on the <a href="/wiki/RV_Rachel_Carson_(2003)" title="RV Rachel Carson (2003)">west coast</a>, owned by <a href="/wiki/MBARI" class="mw-redirect" title="MBARI">MBARI</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> and the other is on <a href="/wiki/RV_Rachel_Carson_(2008)" title="RV Rachel Carson (2008)">the east coast</a>, operated by the University of Maryland. Another vessel of the name, now scrapped, was <a href="/wiki/USS_Crockett_(PG-88)" title="USS Crockett (PG-88)">a former naval vessel</a> obtained and converted by the US EPA. it operated on the Great Lakes. The Florida Keys National Marine Sanctuary also operates a mooring buoy maintenance vessel named the Rachel Carson.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In Woods Hole, Massachusetts, near the US Fisheries building and adjacent to the Marine Biological Laboratory and the <a href="/wiki/Woods_Hole_Oceanographic_Institution" title="Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution">Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution</a>, the Marine Biological Laboratory has erected a life-sized bronze statue of Rachel, sitting on a bench, with a book.</p>
<p>The ceremonial auditorium on the third floor of U.S. EPA's main headquarters, the <a href="/wiki/Ariel_Rios_Building" class="mw-redirect" title="Ariel Rios Building">Ariel Rios Building</a>, is named after Rachel Carson. The Rachel Carson room is just a few feet away from the EPA administrator's office and has been the site of numerous important announcements, including the Clean Air Interstate Rule, since the Agency moved to Ariel Rios in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservation area">conservation areas</a> have been named for Carson as well. Between 1964 and 1990, 650 acres (3&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) near <a href="/wiki/Brookeville,_Maryland" title="Brookeville, Maryland">Brookeville</a> in <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Maryland" title="Montgomery County, Maryland">Montgomery County, Maryland</a> were acquired and set aside as the Rachel Carson Conservation Park, administered by the <a href="/wiki/Maryland-National_Capital_Park_and_Planning_Commission" title="Maryland-National Capital Park and Planning Commission">Maryland-National Capital Park and Planning Commission</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> In 1969, the Coastal Maine National Wildlife Refuge became the <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_National_Wildlife_Refuge" title="Rachel Carson National Wildlife Refuge">Rachel Carson National Wildlife Refuge</a>; expansions will bring the size of the refuge to about 9,125 acres (37&#160;km<sup>2</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> In 1985, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a> renamed one of its <a href="/wiki/Estuarine" class="mw-redirect" title="Estuarine">estuarine</a> reserves in honor of Carson, in <a href="/wiki/Beaufort,_North_Carolina" title="Beaufort, North Carolina">Beaufort</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carson is also a frequent namesake for prizes awarded by philanthropic, educational and scholarly institutions. The <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_Prize_(environmentalist_award)" title="Rachel Carson Prize (environmentalist award)">Rachel Carson Prize</a>, founded in <a href="/wiki/Stavanger" title="Stavanger">Stavanger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> in 1991, is awarded to women who have made a contribution in the field of environmental protection.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Society_for_Environmental_History" title="American Society for Environmental History">American Society for Environmental History</a> has awarded the Rachel Carson Prize for Best Dissertation since 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Since 1998, the <a href="/wiki/Society_for_Social_Studies_of_Science" title="Society for Social Studies of Science">Society for Social Studies of Science</a> has awarded an annual <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_Prize_(academic_book_prize)" title="Rachel Carson Prize (academic book prize)">Rachel Carson Book Prize</a> for "a book length work of social or political relevance in the area of science and technology studies."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Santa_Cruz" title="University of California, Santa Cruz">University of California, Santa Cruz</a>, renamed one of its colleges (formerly known as College Eight) to <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_College" title="Rachel Carson College">Rachel Carson College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Rachel Carson College is the first college at the University to bear a woman's name.</p>
<p>Google created a <a href="/wiki/Google_Doodle" title="Google Doodle">Google Doodle</a> for Carson's 107th birthday on May 27, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Centennial_events">Centennial events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rachel_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Centennial events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rachel_Carson_100th_birthday_crowd.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Rachel_Carson_100th_birthday_crowd.jpg/220px-Rachel_Carson_100th_birthday_crowd.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Rachel_Carson_100th_birthday_crowd.jpg/330px-Rachel_Carson_100th_birthday_crowd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Rachel_Carson_100th_birthday_crowd.jpg/440px-Rachel_Carson_100th_birthday_crowd.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rachel_Carson_100th_birthday_crowd.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The celebration of the 100th anniversary of Carson's birth in <a href="/wiki/Springdale,_Pennsylvania" title="Springdale, Pennsylvania">Springdale, Pennsylvania</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>2007 was the centennial of Carson's birth. On <a href="/wiki/Earth_Day" title="Earth Day">Earth Day</a> (April 22, 2007), <i>Courage for the Earth: Writers, Scientists, and Activists Celebrate the Life and Writing of Rachel Carson</i> was released as "a centennial appreciation of Rachel Carson's brave life and transformative writing." It contained thirteen essays by environmental writers and scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Democratic Senator <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_L._Cardin" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin L. Cardin">Benjamin L. Cardin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> had intended to submit a resolution celebrating Carson for her "legacy of scientific rigor coupled with poetic sensibility" on the 100th anniversary of her birth. The resolution was blocked by Republican Senator <a href="/wiki/Tom_Coburn" title="Tom Coburn">Tom Coburn</a> of <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> who said that "The junk science and stigma surrounding DDT—the cheapest and most effective insecticide on the planet—have finally been jettisoned."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson_Homestead_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="Rachel Carson Homestead Association">Rachel Carson Homestead Association</a> held a May 27 birthday party and sustainable feast at her birthplace and home in <a href="/wiki/Springdale,_Pennsylvania" title="Springdale, Pennsylvania">Springdale, Pennsylvania</a>, and the first Rachel Carson Legacy Conference in Pittsburgh with <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a> as keynote speaker. Both Rachel's Sustainable Feast and the conference continue as annual events.</p>
<p>Also in 2007 American author Ginger Wadsworth wrote a biography of Carson.<sup id="cite_ref-Tierney2007_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tierney2007-122">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_works">List of works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rachel_Carson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: List of works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Under_the_Sea_Wind" title="Under the Sea Wind">Under the Sea Wind</a></i>, 1941, Simon &amp; Schuster, Penguin Group, 1996, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0140253807" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-14-025380-7</a></li>
<li><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://digitalcommons.unl.edu/usfwspubs/6/">"Fishes of the Middle West"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. United States Government Printing Office. 1943.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARachel+Carson&amp;rft.btitle=Fishes+of+the+Middle+West&amp;rft.date=1943&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdigitalcommons.unl.edu%2Fusfwspubs%2F6%2F&amp;rft.pub=United+States+Government+Printing+Office&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>

New York,&#160;NY: <a href="/wiki/Crown_Publishing_Group" title="Crown Publishing Group">Crown Publishers</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-307-46220-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-307-46220-6">978-0-307-46220-6</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARachel+Carson&amp;rft.aufirst=William&amp;rft.aulast=Souder&amp;rft.btitle=On+a+Farther+Shore%3A+The+Life+and+Legacy+of+Rachel+Carson&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-307-46220-6&amp;rft.place=New+York%2C+NY&amp;rft.pub=Crown+Publishers&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Nancy_Koehn" title="Nancy Koehn">Koehn, Nancy</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2012/10/28/business/rachel-carsons-lessons-50-years-after-silent-spring.html?pagewanted=all">"From Calm Leadership, Lasting Change"</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a>,</i> October 27, 2012.</li>

<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=diEDAAAAMBAJ&amp;pg=RA1-PA14&amp;dq=popular+science+1951+why+our+winters&amp;hl=en&amp;ei=9ALETJujGcf_nAe9r73xCQ&amp;sa=X&amp;oi=book_result&amp;ct=result&amp;resnum=1&amp;ved=0CCoQ6AEwAA#v=onepage&amp;q=popular%20science%201951%20why%20our%20winters&amp;f=true">"Why Our Winters Are Getting Warmer", November 1951, <i>Popular Science</i></a> – early article by Rachel Carson about how the ocean's currents affect climate (excerpt from her 1951 book, <i>The Sea Around Us</i>).</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://moviemorlocks.com/2011/02/20/rachel-l-carson-as-interpreted-by-irwin-allen/">(Rachel L. Carson as Interpreted by Irwin Allen – TCM Movie Morlocks on THE SEA AROUND US)</a></li>
